    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'b0b658e9-d435-4715-a3aa-d1fb3b199af8'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d231545729" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>The 1-ethyl-3-methylimidazolium bis(fluorosulfonyl)imide ([EMI][FSI]) and, especially, <i>N</i>-trimethyl-<i>N</i>-butylammonium bis(fluorosulfonyl)imide ([N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI]) have shown very good compatibility towards hard carbon electrode with excellent cycling behavior, which represents one of the best results obtained for hard carbon electrodes in ionic liquid electrolytes, exceeding even that exhibited in organic electrolytes, making them rather appealing for the realization of safe, reliable and highly performing Na-ion cells.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/95305a37-0fb8-464a-9d12-9c8806960efa/cssc202300840-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/95305a37-0fb8-464a-9d12-9c8806960efa/cssc202300840-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d8c01dae-eea9-411f-8093-11c4fea72cd3/cssc202300840-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/95305a37-0fb8-464a-9d12-9c8806960efa/cssc202300840-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d231545733" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Hard carbons (HC) from natural biowaste have been investigated as anodes for sodium-ion batteries in electrolytes based on 1-ethyl-3-methylimidazolium bis(fluorosulfonyl)imide ([EMI][FSI]) and <i>N</i>-trimethyl-<i>N</i>-butylammonium bis(fluorosulfonyl)imide ([N1114][FSI]) ionic liquids. The Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation process has been analyzed by cyclic voltammetry tests, performed at different scan rates for hundreds of cycles, in combination with impedance spectroscopy measurements to decouple bulk and interfacial resistances of the cells. The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient in the HC host has been also evaluated via the Randles-Sevcik equation. Battery performance of HC anodes in the ionic liquid electrolytes has been evaluated in galvanostatic charge/discharge cycles at room temperature. The evolution of the SEI (solid electrochemical interface) layer grown on the HC surface has been carried out by Raman spectroscopy. Overall the sodiation process of the HC host is highly reversible and reproducible. In particular, a capacity retention exceeding 98 % of the initial value has been recorded in[N1114][FSI] electrolytes after more than 1500 cycles with a coulombic efficiency above 99 %, largely beyond standard carbonate-based electrolytes. Raman, transport properties and impedance confirms that ILs disclose the formation of SEI layers with superior ability to support the reversible Na+ intercalation with the possible minor contributions from the EMI+cation.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cssc202300840-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cssc202300840-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered a promising alternative technology to the lithium-ion one for stationary energy storage systems. In fact, sodium has attracted remarkable attention for its larger natural abundance, lower cost and for its chemical similarity compared to lithium.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0001_R_d231545672e393" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Choice Rev. Online">1</a>-<a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0003_R_d231545672e393" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Chem. Rev.">3</a></sup></span> Sodium is an alkaline metal, it shows slightly higher redox potential (−2.71 V vs SHE) in respect to lithium (−3.04 V vs SHE) and allows the use of aluminum as anode current collector instead cupper, which is more expensive and heavier.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0004_R_d231545672e396" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">4</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Among negative electrodes for Na-ion cells, graphite, the most widely used one in Li-ion batteries (LIBs) is unfortunately poorly active:<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0005_R_d231545672e401" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - RSC Adv.">5</a>, <a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0006_R_d231545672e401" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Mater. Today">6</a></sup></span> apparently, the reversible electrochemical intercalation of sodium ions within the graphite layers is limited to the NaC<sub>64</sub> stoichiometry to be compared to the LiC<sub>6</sub> for LIBs.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0007_R_d231545672e408" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">7</a></sup></span> The most effective alternatives to graphite for SIBs anodes are hard carbons (HCs), titanates and conversion materials.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0008_R_d231545672e411" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">8</a>, <a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0009_R_d231545672e411" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Energy Mater.">9</a></sup></span> The so-called non-graphitizable hard carbon attracted great attention thank to the large specific capacity in SIBs, low operative voltage and cycling stability. Moreover, hard carbons can be obtained starting from waste biomasses or natural products<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0010_R_d231545672e415" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ChemSusChem">10</a>-<a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0012_R_d231545672e415" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a></sup></span> and thus, their synthesis almost does not rely on non-renewable raw materials. Overall, HCs are eco-friendly, cheap and, therefore, suitable for large-scale production and commercialization.</p>
         
         <p>HCs, obtained by chemical or thermal process from organic materials,<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0013_R_d231545672e421" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Lignocellul. Precursors Used Synth. Act. Carbon - Charact. Tech. Appl. Wastewater Treat.">13</a></sup></span> show high mechanical hardness and micro-to-meso porosities closely related to the precursor morphology.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0014_R_d231545672e424" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Introduction to Carbon Science">14</a></sup></span> In aprotic SIBs with carbonate-based electrolytes, HC anodes suffer high irreversible capacity losses in the first cycle followed by unsatisfactory coulombic efficiency upon cycling, i. e., in many cases below 99 %<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0015_R_d231545672e427" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ChemElectroChem">15</a>, <a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0016_R_d231545672e427" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Carbon">16</a></sup></span> in Na metal cells.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0017_R_d231545672e430" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Energy Lett.">17</a>, <a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0018_R_d231545672e430" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a></sup></span> This drawback originates from the parasitic reactions involving the electrolyte degradation (reduction) at low potentials, leading to the precipitation of decomposition product and consequent growth-up of the SEI layer (Solid Electrochemical Interface). Unfortunately, the SEI consolidation on HCs for SIBs is not affective like in the case of graphite for LIBs. In fact, previous X-ray photoemission studies proved the evolution of the SEI composition grown on HCs for more than 10 galvanostatic cycles in sodium metal cells.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0019_R_d231545672e433" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ChemElectroChem">19</a></sup></span> This detrimental phenomenon is a serious drawback for SIBs and hinders also the overall safety of the battery.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0017_R_d231545672e437" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Energy Lett.">17</a>, <a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0020_R_d231545672e437" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">20</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>In the last two decades, various strategies have been demonstrated to improve the battery safety and reliability: as an example, by replacing the common organic-based solutions with ceramic, polymeric and ionic liquid (IL) electrolytes.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0021_R_d231545672e442" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Power Sources">21</a>-<a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0023_R_d231545672e442" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Power Sources">23</a></sup></span> ILs are room temperature molten salts and are widely considered as safer electrolyte solvents compared to carbonates thanks to their non-flammability, negligible vapor pressure, good ion transport properties, remarkable thermal/chemical/electrochemical stability. These physicochemical peculiarities have been boosting an impressive raise of the publications about the use of ILs as electrolytes for LIBs<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0024_R_d231545672e445" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24">24</a>-<a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0026_R_d231545672e445" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - MRS Bull.">26</a></sup></span> and, recently, also for SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0027_R_d231545672e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Power Sources">27</a>-<a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0029_R_d231545672e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29">29</a></sup></span> Up to present only few ILs chemistries, mainly based on the pyrrolidinium cation,<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0030_R_d231545672e451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Electrochem. Commun.">30</a>, <a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0031_R_d231545672e451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Electrochim. Acta">31</a></sup></span> have been studied in SIBs despite the innumerable possible variety of potentially applicable ILs. In previous works we studied Na<sup>+</sup>-conducting electrolytes based on different IL families obtaining by coupling the 1-ethyl-3-methyl-imidazolium (EMI), <i>N</i>-trimethyl-<i>N</i>-butyl-ammonium (N<sub>1114</sub>), and <i>N</i>-alkyl-<i>N</i>-methyl-piperidinium (PIP<sub>1A</sub>) cations with bis(fluorosulfonyl)imide (FSI), bis(trifluoromethylsulfonyl)imide (TFSI) and (nonafluorobutylsulfonyl)(trifluoromethylsulfonyl) imide (IM<sub>14</sub>) anions.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0032_R_d231545672e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Appl. Sci.">32</a>, <a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0033_R_d231545672e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Appl. Sci.">33</a></sup></span> These ILs have been synthesized and, successively characterized in electrolyte formulations with sodium salts. Overall, we demonstrated that [EMI][FSI] and[N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI] electrolytes are promising candidates for SIB systems thanks to their fast ion transport properties (&gt;1 mS cm<sup>−1</sup>), even at low temperature (−10 °C), wide electrochemical stability window (extending from 0.1 to 4.5 V vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na°) and the ability of reversibly intercalating sodium ions in the HCs electrodes.</p>
         
         <p>Here we extend out previous studies and we focus on the identification of the chemical/electrochemical origin of the excellent performance of HC anodes using [EMI][FSI] and[N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI] electrolytes. We exploit cyclic voltammetry, impedance spectroscopy and Raman spectroscopy to outline the impact of the ILs based electrolytes on the SEI stability, the changes in the Na<sup>+</sup> transport properties and the surface alterations of the HCs upon cycling, with the aim of gaining information about the SEI layer<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0034_R_d231545672e489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Nat. Commun.">34</a>, <a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0035_R_d231545672e489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">35</a></sup></span> on the electrode surface. Furthermore, we experimentally demonstrate the outstanding capacity retention of HC electrodes in ILs-based electrolytes in room temperature galvanostatic charge/discharge measurements in Na/HC cells for thousands of cycles.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cssc202300840-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cssc202300840-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>In previous works from our groups,<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0032_R_d231545672e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Appl. Sci.">32</a>, <a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0033_R_d231545672e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Appl. Sci.">33</a></sup></span> we analyzed different Na<sup>+</sup>-conducting IL electrolytes: a summary of the most relevant physicochemical properties is presented in Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#cssc202300840-tbl-0001">1</a>. The [EMI][FSI] and[N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI] based electrolytes show the most promising features: in particular, a remarkable conductivity (&gt;10<sup>−3</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup>) even at low temperature (−10 °C) couples with an extended electrochemical stability window in parallel with the ability of allowing reversible intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> cations in HCs electrodes.
</p>

         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cssc202300840-sec-0003">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cssc202300840-sec-0003-title"> Sodium intercalation process</h3>
            
            <p>Cyclic voltammograms are shown in Figure <a href="#cssc202300840-fig-0001">1</a> at different scan rates (panels A and B) and after prolonged cycles (panels C and D) for both the [EMI][FSI] and[N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI] based electrolytes. Current values have been normalized with respect to the HC mass loading of electrodes (specific current). A broad and intense feature, related to the electrochemical passivation layer (SEI) formation on the HC surface<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0019_R_d231545672e820" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ChemElectroChem">19</a>, <a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0036_R_d231545672e820" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Electrochim. Acta">36</a></sup></span> and located around 0.6 V vs the Na<sup>+</sup>/Na redox couple, can be observed for both IL electrolyte formulations during the 1<sup>st</sup> cathodic scan (black trace) conducted at low scan rate (0.05 mV s<sup>−1</sup>). This peak disappears in the following cycles, suggesting negligible degradation processes of the electrolytes. Generally speaking, sodium insertion into the HC electrode occurs through a two-step storage mechanism: i) Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation into graphene layers above 200 mV vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na and ii) Na<sup>+</sup> insertion into micropores formed by the pseudographitic domains with an oxidation state close to that of Na metal, which is detected below 200 mV.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0006_R_d231545672e836" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Mater. Today">6</a>, <a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0008_R_d231545672e836" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">8</a>, <a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0037_R_d231545672e836" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Challenges">37</a>, <a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0038_R_d231545672e836" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Mater.">38</a></sup></span> The presence of two well-defined features around 0.15 V and 0.7 V (related to this two-step mechanism insertion) can be observed, in both electrolytes, during the first cycles at low scan rate in line with the expected sodium ion incorporation mechanism. A good reversibility of the sodiation is observed even at high scan rates and during prolonged cycles. The increase of the scan rate results in the expected intensity enhancement of the current <i>vs</i>. voltage profiles (both in the anodic and cathodic branches) with only modest shift of the maximum value, likely due to diffusion following the Randles-Sevcik equation<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0039_R_d231545672e842" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">39</a>, <a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0040_R_d231545672e842" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">40</a></sup></span> (see below). The 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI] cells show larger value of the specific current related to the electrochemical processes with respect to the 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[EMI][FSI] ones, indicating larger Na<sup>+</sup> equivalent exchange and, therefore, a larger capacity delivered by the sodium (de)intercalation.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cssc202300840-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/03ee3408-ad4f-4361-a6ae-9955f93b2aec/cssc202300840-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/03ee3408-ad4f-4361-a6ae-9955f93b2aec/cssc202300840-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3df683a4-1733-4c46-9c39-d5fee0ac678f/cssc202300840-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/03ee3408-ad4f-4361-a6ae-9955f93b2aec/cssc202300840-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cssc202300840-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcssc.202300840" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Cyclic voltammograms of Na/HC cells containing 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[EMI][FSI] (panels A and C) and 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI] (panels B and D) electrolytes at different scan rates (panel A and B) and for prolonged cycles with a scan rate of 1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> (panel B and D). T=20 °C.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Prolonged cyclic voltammetry analysis has been performed in challenging conditions (500 cycles at 1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>) to further demonstrate the reversibility of the sodiation processes in hard carbon electrodes. The results, displayed in Figure <a href="#cssc202300840-fig-0001">1C</a> ([EMI][FSI] electrolyte) and 1D ([N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI] electrolyte), show for both electrolytes well-defined current-potential profile around 0.5 V (cathodic scan) and 0.6 V (anodic scan). Furthermore, the excellent reproducibility of voltammograms cycle-by-cycle confirms the outstanding reversibility of the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation, even after hundreds of cycles for both IL electrolytes. This evidence is a possible clue of outstanding retention capacity for the hard carbon electrodes in any electrochemical cycling test. The Na/HC cells containing the 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI] electrolyte show stronger voltammetry curves with higher specific current value, indicating a larger amount of charge involved in the sodiation process.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cssc202300840-sec-0004">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cssc202300840-sec-0004-title"> Evaluation of the sodium diffusion coefficient</h3>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">The increase in the current value recorded in the CV tests reflects the raise in the scan rate, i. e., in line with the Randles-Sevcik equation.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0039_R_d231545672e899" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">39</a>, <a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0040_R_d231545672e899" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">40</a></sup></span> All voltammograms (recorded at different scan rates) have been analyzed to evaluate the diffusion coefficient <i>D</i><sub>0</sub> (cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>) of the sodium ions in the HC anodes by means Equation (1),<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0041_R_d231545672e909" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Collect. Czech. Chem. Commun.">41</a>, <a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0042_R_d231545672e909" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Trans. Faraday Soc.">42</a></sup></span> which describes the reversible processes taking place in redox reactions, where the mass transport is assumed occurring only through a 3D diffusion phenomenon:
               <div class="inline-equation" id="cssc202300840-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/adb2e80e-0171-41c9-94a7-ecbd74031033/cssc202300840-math-0001.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:18645631:media:cssc202300840:cssc202300840-math-0001" title="urn:x-wiley:18645631:media:cssc202300840:cssc202300840-math-0001" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
               </div>
            
            <p>where <i>n</i> is the number of electron equivalents exchanged during the reversible process, <i>F</i> is the Faraday constant, <i>A</i> (cm<sup>2</sup>) is the active surface area of the HC working electrode, <i>C</i> (mol cm<sup>−3</sup>) is the concentration of the diffusing species, <i>v</i> (V s<sup>−1</sup>) is the scan rate, <i>R</i> is the universal gas constant and <i>T</i> is the absolute temperature (<i>K</i>). The peak current (<i>i</i><sub>p</sub>) values, related to the <i>C</i> parameter and associated to the sodium insertion-extraction with the respective redox potential (<i>E</i><sub>p</sub>), are summarized in Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#cssc202300840-tbl-0002">2</a>. The peak current ratio of the anodic (<i>i</i><sub>pa</sub>) and cathodic (<i>i</i><sub>pc</sub>) scans is almost equal to unity in both IL electrolyte formulations, suggesting reversible intercalation process and no chemical reaction coupled with the electron transfer.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0042_R_d231545672e965" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Trans. Faraday Soc.">42</a></sup></span>
               </p>
            
            <div class="article-table-content" id="cssc202300840-tbl-0002">
               
               
               
               
               <header class="article-table-caption"><span class="table-caption__label">Table  2.
                    </span>Anodic (<i>i</i><sub>pa</sub>) and cathodic (<i>i</i><sub>pc</sub>) peak current values, and related anodic (<i>E</i><sub>pa</sub>) and cathodic (E<sub><i>pc</i></sub>) peak voltage, determined at different scan rates for Na/HC cells using the 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[EMI][FSI] and 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI] electrolytes. <i>T</i>=20 °C.</header>

               </div>

               <div class="article-section__table-source"></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>Figure <a href="#cssc202300840-fig-0002">2</a> plots the <i>i</i><sub>pa</sub> and <i>i</i><sub>pc</sub> current values against the square root of the scan rate for Na/HC cells in 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[EMI][FSI] and 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI] electrolytes. A straight-line dependence (crossing the axes origin) is observed, supporting for the diffusive nature of the sodium insertion in the hard carbon electrode. The <i>D</i><sub>0</sub> parameter was calculated through the slope <i>i</i><sub>p</sub><i>/v</i><sup><i>1/2</i></sup> (proportional to the square root of the diffusion coefficient),<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0043_R_d231545672e1397" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43">43</a>-<a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0045_R_d231545672e1397" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Electrochim. Acta">45</a></sup></span> obtained by linear fitting (Figure S2 in the Supporting Information). Comparable diffusion coefficients were found for both IL Na-electrolytes, with a moderately higher value ((4.9±0.3)×10<sup>−9</sup> vs. (3.5±0.2)×10<sup>−9</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>) for the 0.1NaTFSI- 0.9[EMI][FSI] electrolyte formulation likely due to the faster ion transport properties of the [EMI][FSI] ionic liquid.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0032_R_d231545672e1409" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Appl. Sci.">32</a></sup></span> The error on the <i>D</i> values was found to be equal to 5 %. It is to note that they are one order of magnitude lower with respect to those reported in literature for organic electrolytes and determined through different techniques,<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0046_R_d231545672e1414" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Electrochim. Acta">46</a>-<a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0048_R_d231545672e1414" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - ACS Phys. Chem. Au">48</a></sup></span> likely due to the higher viscosity of the IL electrolyte medium.
</p>

               <figure class="figure" id="cssc202300840-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e4abfd80-a860-4651-8d0a-1156a456890a/cssc202300840-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e4abfd80-a860-4651-8d0a-1156a456890a/cssc202300840-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/69294020-68b9-4002-8ec6-85ffce819e59/cssc202300840-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e4abfd80-a860-4651-8d0a-1156a456890a/cssc202300840-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cssc202300840-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcssc.202300840" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>

         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cssc202300840-sec-0005">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cssc202300840-sec-0005-title"> Impedance measurements</h3>
            
            <p>The AC responses, recorded after each CV tests run at different scan rates (Figure <a href="#cssc202300840-fig-0001">1</a>), of Na/HC cells in 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[EMI][FSI] (panel A) and 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI] (panel B) electrolytes are reported in Figure <a href="#cssc202300840-fig-0003">3</a> as Nyquist plots. A medium-high frequency (10 kHz–100 Hz) semicircle pattern, related to the overall interfacial resistance,<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0049_R_d231545672e1463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - ACS Omega">49</a>-<a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0051_R_d231545672e1463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51">51</a></sup></span> is observed, followed by a straight line (100–1 Hz) which is related to the HC electrode capacitance.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0049_R_d231545672e1466" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - ACS Omega">49</a>-<a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0051_R_d231545672e1466" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51">51</a></sup></span> The high-frequency intercept of the semicircle with the real axis gives the bulk resistance of the IL-based electrolyte. The AC responses have been fitted by a proper equivalent circuit model (Figure S1) where <i>R</i><sub>bulk</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>int</sub> are the electrolyte and overall interfacial resistance, respectively, <i>Q</i><sub>dl</sub> is the constant phase element taking on account for the double layer capacitance and C<sub>l</sub> is the electrode limit capacitance.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0049_R_d231545672e1482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - ACS Omega">49</a>-<a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0051_R_d231545672e1482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51">51</a></sup></span> <i>R</i><sub>bulk</sub> is found to be around 3 and 25 Ω cm<sup>2</sup> (at 20 °C) for 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[EMI][FSI] and 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI] cells, respectively, i. e., in good agreement with the ionic conductivity values reported in previous paper<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0032_R_d231545672e1492" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Appl. Sci.">32</a></sup></span> supporting for faster ion transport properties for the [EMI][FSI] electrolyte (8.6×10<sup>−3</sup> vs 1.8×10<sup>−3</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup>). As summarized in Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#cssc202300840-tbl-0003">3</a> and evidenced in Figure S3, no relevant increase of the <i>R</i><sub>bulk</sub> value is observed during CV cycles run at increasing scan rates, suggesting no appreciable degradation processes on the electrode surfaces in both IL electrolytes.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cssc202300840-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5cab6da9-e272-452e-a2c4-9967e3ae725c/cssc202300840-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/5cab6da9-e272-452e-a2c4-9967e3ae725c/cssc202300840-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/78592d0e-effd-4d78-8c4e-7c8c067e57dc/cssc202300840-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5cab6da9-e272-452e-a2c4-9967e3ae725c/cssc202300840-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

            <p>Figures <a href="#cssc202300840-fig-0003">3C</a> and <a href="#cssc202300840-fig-0003">3D</a> display the Nyquist plots, obtained after prolonged CV analysis (Figure <a href="#cssc202300840-fig-0001">1C</a>–<a href="#cssc202300840-fig-0001">D</a>), related to 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[EMI][FSI] and 0.1NaTFSI-0.9 N<sub>1114</sub>FSI cells, respectively. Again, no variation of the AC response shape was observed for both Na/HC cells. The <i>R</i><sub>bulk</sub> value, recorded after 500 cycles, is close to the initial one, confirming the absence of degradation phenomena within the IL electrolytes. No practical increase of the <i>R</i><sub>int</sub> resistance (close to 180 Ω cm<sup>2</sup>) is seen for the [EMI][FSI] cells, indicating stable electrolyte/electrode interface during the CV tests. Conversely, a marked decrease (from 260 to 180 Ω cm<sup>2</sup>) is observed for the[N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI] ones, suggesting a morphological and/or structural gradual variation of SEI on the electrode surface and upholding the behavior displayed Figure <a href="#cssc202300840-fig-0003">3A</a> and <a href="#cssc202300840-fig-0003">B</a>. Therefore, the N<sub>1114</sub><sup>+</sup> cation seems to play a favorable role for the SEI composition on the HC and Na surfaces.</p>
            
            <p>Figure <a href="#cssc202300840-fig-0004">4</a> displays the AC response of Na/0.1NaTFSI-0.9[EMI][FSI]/HC and Na/0.1NaTFSI-0.9[N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI]/HC cells recorded before (panel A) and after (panel C) the sodiation process of the HC electrode occurred during the 1<sup>st</sup> cathodic scan (panel B). A further depressed semicircle (Figure <a href="#cssc202300840-fig-0004">4C</a>) appears in the medium-low frequency range after the first cathodic scan, due to the growth-up of a passive layer at the HC/electrolyte interface;<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0049_R_d231545672e1827" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - ACS Omega">49</a>, <a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0052_R_d231545672e1827" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">52</a></sup></span> conversely, no change of electrolyte resistance is seen. The evolution of the <i>R</i><sub>bulk</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>int</sub> parameters against the scan rates is displayed in Figure S3. A progressive reduction of the interfacial resistance during the CV tests is observed, i. e., from 240 to 110 Ω cm<sup>2</sup> and from about 270 to 145 Ω cm<sup>2</sup> for the 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[EMI][FSI] and 0.1NaTFSI-0.9 N<sub>1114</sub>FSI cells, respectively, due to an improvement of the electrolyte/HC interface probably ascribable to the increase in electronic conductivity of the HC active material during the sodiation process, which promotes the electrochemical reaction kinetics.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0053_R_d231545672e1844" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53">53</a></sup></span> Finally, the <i>Q</i><sub>dl</sub> element is found to be of the order of 10<sup>−6</sup> F, i. e., as expected for double layer capacitance in lithium or sodium battery systems.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0049_R_d231545672e1852" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - ACS Omega">49</a>-<a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0051_R_d231545672e1852" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51">51</a></sup></span>
               </p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cssc202300840-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b8d004fa-233c-449c-8d98-fcb4d8736e89/cssc202300840-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b8d004fa-233c-449c-8d98-fcb4d8736e89/cssc202300840-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8ca3ec63-a000-418d-86cb-73a5f91435cc/cssc202300840-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b8d004fa-233c-449c-8d98-fcb4d8736e89/cssc202300840-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cssc202300840-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcssc.202300840" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>AC response of Na/0.1NaTFSI-0.9[EMI][FSI]/HC and Na/0.1NaTFSI-0.9[N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI]/HC cells before (panel A) and after (panel C) sodiation process of the HC electrode occurred during the 1<sup>st</sup> cathodic scan (panel B). The insets in panels A and C magnify the high to middle frequency region. Frequency range: 10 kHz–1 Hz. Voltage amplitude: Δ<i>V</i>=10 mV. <i>T</i>=20 °C.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>

            </section>

         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cssc202300840-sec-0006">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cssc202300840-sec-0006-title"> Cycling behavior</h3>
            
            <p>The electrochemical performances of hard carbon electrodes in 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[EMI][FSI] and 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI] electrolytes have been investigated by galvanostatic cycling measurements at different current rates. The voltage-capacity profiles (panels A and B of Figure <a href="#cssc202300840-fig-0005">5</a>) display the expected two-step sodium intercalation process, i. e., a sloping curve at higher voltage (related to the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation into pseudo-graphitic regions) from the OCV until 200 mV vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na°, followed by a reduced pseudo-plateau around 100 mV as a result of the ion-electron absorption inside the HC electrode microporosity.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0008_R_d231545672e1904" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">8</a>, <a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0054_R_d231545672e1904" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54">54</a></sup></span> Above 200 mV, 67 % (305 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and 77 % (360 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) of the overall capacity are delivered in the [EMI][FSI] and[N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI] cells, respectively, during the first Na-insertion whereas the remaining fraction (33 and 24 %, respectively) is discharged in the pseudo-plateau around 100 mV, according to the data reported in literature.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0019_R_d231545672e1914" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ChemElectroChem">19</a>, <a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0054_R_d231545672e1914" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54">54</a></sup></span> A large irreversible capacity (<i>Q</i><sub>irr</sub>) is observed during the first discharge, i. e., missing in the following cycles, due to the SEI formation onto the HC electrode,<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0008_R_d231545672e1921" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">8</a></sup></span> in agreement with the results reported in previous paper<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0033_R_d231545672e1924" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Appl. Sci.">33</a></sup></span> for these IL electrolyte formulations. For instance, a <i>Q</i><sub>irr</sub> value levelling 61 % (270 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) is recorded for the [N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI] cells (Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#cssc202300840-tbl-0002">2</a>), i. e., comparable with that observed in organic electrolytes,<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0019_R_d231545672e1938" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ChemElectroChem">19</a>, <a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0055_R_d231545672e1938" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Nano Energy">55</a>, <a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0056_R_d231545672e1938" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - npj Comput. Mater.">56</a></sup></span> increasing up to 73 % (335 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) for the [EMI][FSI] electrolyte (Figure <a href="#cssc202300840-fig-0005">5D</a>). No appreciable change of the discharge/charge voltage profiles is seen in passing from 0.1 C to 1 C (panel B), indicating fast kinetics of the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation process in conjunction with the good ion transport properties of the IL electrolytes. Interesting reversible capacities are exhibited with good rate capability (Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#cssc202300840-tbl-0004">4</a>): for instance, 127, 115, 96, 79 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> ([EMI][FSI]) and 175, 163, 135, 109 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> ([N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI]) are observed at 0.1 C, 0.2 C, 0.5 C and 1 C, respectively, i. e., comparable with the values reported for organic electrolytes.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0019_R_d231545672e1958" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ChemElectroChem">19</a></sup></span> Therefore, around 64–66 % of the initial (nominal) capacity (0.1 C) is displayed at 1 C (panel E). The coulombic efficiency quickly overcomes 95 % after few cycles in both IL formulations, levelling at 99 % (panel C) even during very prolonged cycling tests (over 2000 cycles). By increasing the current rate down to 0.2 C from 1 C (panel C), the capacity returns to the value initially exhibited (at 0.2 C), once more confirming the very good reversibility of the sodium intercalation process in HC electrodes. It is to be noted that an excellent capacity retention is shown, i. e., 87 % ([EMI][FSI] electrolyte) and 98.2 % ([N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI]) of the initial capacity are delivered after 1500 consecutive cycles at 0.2 C, corresponding to a fading lower than 0.0090 % ([EMI][FSI]) and 0.0012 ([N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI]) per cycle. This superior cycling behavior, largely exceeding that reported for organic carbonate-based electrolytes<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0019_R_d231545672e1966" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ChemElectroChem">19</a>, <a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0053_R_d231545672e1966" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53">53</a>, <a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0057_R_d231545672e1966" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - J. Mater. Chem. A">57</a></sup></span> has never been reported to far in the literature. In fact, several electrolytes were investigated in other works,<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0008_R_d231545672e1969" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">8</a>, <a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0058_R_d231545672e1969" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Electrochim. Acta">58</a>, <a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0059_R_d231545672e1969" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">59</a></sup></span> based on PC and/or ethylene carbonate (EC), dimethyl carbonate (DMC), diethyl carbonate (DEC) solvents, etc., in combination with NaClO<sub>4</sub>, NaTFSI or NaPF<sub>6</sub> as sodium salts, which show values of reversible capacity close to those reported for the[N1114][FSI]-cell but with significant capacity fading upon cycling and higher initial irreversible capacity, demonstrating that the choice of the electrolyte plays a key role for good battery performances. Other innovative and efficient approaches were evaluated to boost the rate performances and the specific capacity of hard carbon anodes, such as doping with heteroatoms (i. e., pyridinic N and thiophene S) through different techniques,<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0060_R_d231545672e1976" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Carbon">60</a>, <a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0061_R_d231545672e1976" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Nanomaterials">61</a></sup></span> which allowed to enhance the sodium storage capacity. So, different strategies can be followed to raise the efficiency of hard carbon as anode, improving its structure and/or designing suitable electrolytes. The superior performances shown in our case are likely related to the good compatibility of FSI-based ionic
</p>

            <p>liquids, especially[N1114][FSI], with respect to the standard commercial battery solvents towards the HC electrodes. This is confirmed also in consideration of the experimental results recorded on a Na metal cell with 1 M NaTFSI in PC+3wt. %FEC cycled in the analogues conditions (Figure <a href="#cssc202300840-fig-0005">5C</a>). Turning back to ILs-based electrolytes, the reversible capacity vs current density dependence, displayed in Figure <a href="#cssc202300840-fig-0005">5E</a>, do not show any step decay up to 1 C, suggesting that the IL electrolytes are not affected by relevant diffusion phenomena up to this current rate. To summarize, the Na/0.1NaTFSI-0.9[N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI]/HC cell configuration shows better performance, in terms of capacity, retention, rate capability and coulombic efficiency, than that based on [EMI][FSI].</p>

         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cssc202300840-sec-0007">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cssc202300840-sec-0007-title"> Raman characterization</h3>
            
            <p>In order to better understand the structural alteration of hard carbon electrodes during the electrochemical insertion/removal of Na<sup>+</sup> ions in different electrolytes, micro-Raman spectroscopy measurements have been carried out on pristine and post mortem samples collected from cells. Figure <a href="#cssc202300840-fig-0006">6</a> shows the Raman spectra, recorded both in the full discharged (sodiated) and charged (desodiated) state after the 1<sup>st</sup> and 10<sup>th</sup> cycle, of HC electrodes in the 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[EMI][FSI] and 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI] electrolytes, while the Raman spectra related to the pristine HC sample was displayed in Figure S4. The Raman spectra have been analysed according to the model proposed by Sadezky <i>et al</i>.,<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0060_R_d231545672e2189" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Carbon">60</a></sup></span> which identifies the main components of the hard carbon signals at ~1595 cm<sup>−1</sup> (G band), ~1340 cm<sup>−1</sup> (D1 band), ~1630 cm<sup>−1</sup> (D2 band), ~1500 cm<sup>−1</sup> (D3 band), and ~1200 cm<sup>−1</sup> (D4 band), respectively. The curve-fitted Raman spectrum of the pristine HC electrode (Figure S4) shows the D1, D3, and G bands as the main components, related to microcrystalline graphitic domains, amorphous carbon, and extended graphitic domains, respectively,<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0062_R_d231545672e2203" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - J. Ind. Eng. Chem.">62</a>, <a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0063_R_d231545672e2203" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - Adv. Energy Mater.">63</a></sup></span> confirming the structure observed in the previous work<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0019_R_d231545672e2206" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ChemElectroChem">19</a></sup></span> through measurements of X-ray diffraction (XRD), Raman spectroscopy, N<sub>2</sub> adsorption (BET) and scanning electron microscopy (SEM). Where the synthesized HC was found to have the characteristic “falling card structural motif”,<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0064_R_d231545672e2211" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 64 - ACS Appl. Energ. Mater.">64</a></sup></span> which consists of randomly graphene sheets packed with large amounts of micropores. The I<sub>G</sub>/I<sub>D3</sub> area ratio, representing the degree of graphitization,<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0065_R_d231545672e2219" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 65 - Adv. Energy Mater.">65</a></sup></span> of the pristine hard carbon is equal to 0.42, indicating a highly disordered structure. In Figure <a href="#cssc202300840-fig-0006">6</a> (panel A) the Raman spectra of sodiated and desodiated electrodes cycled in the[N1114][FSI]-based electrolyte show differences in the profile shape, owed to the sodium insertion. The rise in intensity of the D4 component at ~1150 cm<sup>−1</sup> results in the appearance of a left shoulder with respect to the main D1 band component. Moreover, a slight rise of the D3 contribution at ~1500 cm<sup>−1</sup>, leading to a more pronounced coalescence between D and G bands, is observed in the case of sodiated samples. These bands are likely attributed to the amorphous carbon (D3) and the disordered graphitic lattice (D4).<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0062_R_d231545672e2229" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - J. Ind. Eng. Chem.">62</a>, <a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0063_R_d231545672e2229" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - Adv. Energy Mater.">63</a></sup></span> No shift of the D1 and G bands is shown (i. e., comparing the spectra of the sodiated and de-sodiated electrode), suggesting that sodium is likely incorporated through an adsorption mechanism on the hard carbon surfaces and defects, or pore filling, whereas intercalation occurs in a secondary process.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0066" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0066_R_d231545672e2233" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 66 - ECS Proc. Vol.">66</a></sup></span>
               </p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cssc202300840-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/dd077d37-08ab-4344-ae65-deaf20f92b27/cssc202300840-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/dd077d37-08ab-4344-ae65-deaf20f92b27/cssc202300840-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7fc0f033-9ba6-49c0-9a5a-ef4d760dbd5a/cssc202300840-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/dd077d37-08ab-4344-ae65-deaf20f92b27/cssc202300840-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cssc202300840-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcssc.202300840" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Raman spectra, recorded after the 1<sup>st</sup> and the 10<sup>th</sup> cycle, of HC electrodes (in the full-discharged and full-charged state) in 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI] (a) and 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[EMI][FSI] (c) electrolytes. Fitting curves of Raman spectra of HC electrodes (in the full-charged state) after the 1<sup>st</sup> and 10<sup>th</sup> cycle in 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI] (b) and 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[EMI][FSI] (d) electrolytes.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Conversely, a modification of the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion mechanism is noted in the 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[EMI][FSI] electrolyte: a D band shift is detected for the sodiated electrode after the 1<sup>st</sup> cycle, whereas both D and G bands look shifted after the 10<sup>th</sup> cycle. This suggests that Na<sup>+</sup> insertion firstly involves only the microcrystalline graphitic domains (D1 band), whereas the extended graphitic domains (G band) are subsequently involved as the cycle number increases. In addition, the shift of the D and G bands demonstrates an increase of the d-spacing between the graphene layers owed to intercalation.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0067" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0067_R_d231545672e2283" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 67 - Nature">67</a></sup></span> Avoiding, however, any speculation, it is possible that such a behavior is originated not only by Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation, but also by the simultaneous co-intercalation of the EMI<sup>+</sup> cation, as reported in previous paper,<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0065_R_d231545672e2291" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 65 - Adv. Energy Mater.">65</a>-<a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0067" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0067_R_d231545672e2291" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 67 - Nature">67</a></sup></span> likely because of its reduced size and planarity.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0024_R_d231545672e2294" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24">24</a></sup></span> The fitting of the Raman spectra, recorded for the desodiated electrodes after the 1<sup>st</sup> and 10<sup>th</sup> cycle, allows to investigate the modification occurring in the hard carbon structure upon cycling. The results, reported in panels B ([N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI] electrolyte) and D ([EMI][FSI] electrolyte), clearly show no significant shift (Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#cssc202300840-tbl-0005">5</a>) and/or intensity variation of the Raman feature in[N1114][FSI] electrolyte, supporting for good structural stability. The <i>I</i><sub>G</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>D3</sub> (where <i>I</i><sub>G</sub> and <i>I</i><sub>D3</sub> represent the area comprised by the G and D3 features with respect to the real axis) area ratio is seen to decrease from 1.22 to 1.06 upon cycling, however significantly higher than that of the pristine electrode, suggesting that the HC structure evolves likely enhancing the graphite-like character in the[N1114][FSI] electrolyte formulation. Conversely, a relevant shift of the D<sub>3</sub> and D<sub>4</sub> bands, both attributed to amorphous carbon, is detected for the electrode cycled in 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[EMI][FSI]. The <i>I</i><sub>G</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>D3</sub> area ratio ranges from 0.51 to 0.43, i. e., very close to the graphitization degree of the pristine HC. Therefore, the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation process in [EMI][FSI] electrolyte involves microcrystalline (D1 band) or extended (G band) graphitic domains without leading to deep structural modifications, while the amorphous carbon region is more significantly affected. As the most relevant shift is related to the D4 band, i. e., attributed in particular to C−H termination groups and to the contribution of adsorbed molecules,<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0006_R_d231545672e2333" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Mater. Today">6</a></sup></span> it can be argued that one of the main causes of the observed variations is likely a change in the SEI layer morphology or composition. Work is in progress in our labs to investigate this issue.
</p>
            
            <div class="article-table-content" id="cssc202300840-tbl-0005">
               
               
               
               
               <header class="article-table-caption"><span class="table-caption__label">Table  5.
                    </span>Positions of the G, D1, D3 and D4 bands of HC full-charged electrodes after cycling in 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI] and 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[EMI][FSI] electrolyte.</header>

         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cssc202300840-sec-0008">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cssc202300840-sec-0008-title"> Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>The Na<sup>+</sup>-intercalation process of hard carbon (HC) electrodes in 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[EMI][FSI] and 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI] ionic liquid (IL) electrolytes was investigated through repeated cyclic voltammetry (CV) tests paired with impedance spectroscopy (PEIS) analysis. High reversibility and reproducibility of the sodiation process were observed in both electrolyte formulations. More defined CV profiles were exhibited in 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI] electrolyte, indicating a larger charge amount involved in the sodiation process. This trend is in agreement with the charge-discharge test results, where the Na/HC cells in the[N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI] electrolyte show excellent cycling performances with capacity retention exceeding 98 % of the initial value (175 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) after more than 1500 cycles with a coulombic efficiency above 99 %. This superior cyclability represents one of the best, if not the best, results obtained for hard carbon electrodes in ionic liquid electrolytes, exceeding even that exhibited in organic electrolytes.</p>
         
         <p>The chemical/electrochemical stability of the [EMI][FSI] and[N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI] ionic liquids toward HC electrodes was confirmed by PEIS measurements, where no appreciable change of the electrolyte resistance was recorded during the CV measurements. The interface resistance is seen to be reduced upon cycling, likely due to the improvement of the electrolyte/electrode interface arising from the electric conductivity increase of the HC electrodes with the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion. The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>0</sub>), evaluated through the Randles-Sevcik equation, was found to be 4.9×10<sup>−9</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> and 3.5×10<sup>−9</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> in the 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[EMI][FSI] and 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI] electrolytes, respectively. These values are one order of magnitude lower with respect to those reported in the literature and referred to organic electrolytes, likely ascribable to the slower ion transport properties of the ILs. Raman measurements revealed a more favorable Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation process for the[N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI] electrolyte whereas a co-insertion of the IL cation can occur in [EMI][FSI], thus depleting the HC electrode performance. To summarize, the [EMI][FSI] and, especially, [N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI] electrolytes have shown very good compatibility towards hard carbon electrodes, resulting rather appealing for the realization of safer, more reliable, and highly performant Na-ion cells.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cssc202300840-sec-0009">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cssc202300840-sec-0009-title"> Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cssc202300840-sec-0010">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cssc202300840-sec-0010-title"> Materials and methods</h3>
            
            <p>1-ethyl-3-methylimidazolium bis(fluorosulfonyl)imide ([EMI][FSI]) and <i>N</i>-trimethyl-<i>N</i>-butyl-ammonium bis(fluorosulfonyl)imide ([N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI]) were synthesized and purified according to an eco-friendly route reported elsewhere<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0032_R_d231545672e2586" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Appl. Sci.">32</a>, <a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0036_R_d231545672e2586" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Electrochim. Acta">36</a>, <a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0037_R_d231545672e2586" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Challenges">37</a></sup></span> [EMI][FSI] and[N1114][FSI] were combined with the sodium bis(trifluoromethanesulfonyl)imide (NaTFSI, Solvionic, 99.5 wt. %) salt. The binary NaTFSI/IL electrolytes were prepared by dissolving (magnetic stirring) the proper amount of sodium salt in the ionic liquid at room temperature (NaTFSI/IL molar ratio=1 : 9).<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0030_R_d231545672e2589" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Electrochem. Commun.">30</a>-<a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0033_R_d231545672e2589" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Appl. Sci.">33</a></sup></span> The 1 M NaTFSI in PC+3 wt. % FEC was purchased by Solvionic.</p>
            
            <p>The hard carbon material was synthetized (starting from an organic precursor) according to a route described in detail elsewhere.<span><sup><a href="#cssc202300840-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc202300840-bib-0019_R_d231545672e2595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ChemElectroChem">19</a></sup></span> Composite electrodes were prepared by casting <i>N</i>-methyl pyrrolidone slurries of the HC (75 % in weight) mixed with super-P carbon (15 wt. %) and polyvinylidene difluoride binder (10 wt. %) on 20 μm thick Al foils. The mean mass loading of the electrodes was about 1.0 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>, corresponding to a theoretical capacity of 0.3 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> (300 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). The HC electrodes were punched in disks with 10 mm diameter, dried overnight under dynamic vacuum at 120 °C and stored in an Ar-filled glove box (Jacomex, Dagneux, France, O<sub>2</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>O level &lt;1 ppm). All materials as well as the cell manufacturing were stored and handled in an Ar-filled glove box.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cssc202300840-sec-0011">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cssc202300840-sec-0011-title"> Electrochemical measurements</h3>
            
            <p>The Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/deintercalation process in the HC active material and the SEI formation onto the electrodes were studied by cyclic voltammetric (CV) analysis paired with potentiostatic electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (PEIS) measurements. CV tests at different scan rates and run for prolonged cycles, associated with PEIS measurements, were performed on 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[EMI][FSI] and 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI] electrolytes in Na metal cells using HC as the working electrode material. Coin cells (CR2032) have been assembled (i. e., Na/0.1NaTFSI-0.9IL/HC galvanic chain) by coupling a hard-carbon working electrode, two glass fiber disk (16 mm diameter, 100 μm thickness) separators (Whatman TM, Maidstone, UK) soaked with 150 μL of electrolyte, and a sodium metal (American Elements, Los Angeles, CA, USA) disk (10 mm diameter, 100–200 μm thickness) counter electrode. The CV tests were carried out between 0.01 to 2.2 V vs the Na<sup>+</sup>/Na° redox couple at increasing scan rates, i. e., from 0.05 to 1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> (running four consecutive cycles for each selected scan rate). Finally, the Na/HC cells were subjected to 500 consecutive CV cycles carried out at 1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. Impedance measurements were run on the as manufactured cells, at the end of each CV family (at increasing scan rate) and after prolonged CV test, in the frequency range from 10 kHz to 0.1 Hz with 10 mV amplitude voltage.</p>
            
            <p>The performances of HC material in Na metal cells, using the 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[EMI][FSI], 0.1NaTFSI-0.9[N<sub>1114</sub>][FSI] and 1 M NaTFSI in PC+3 wt. % FEC (for comparison purpose) electrolytes, were evaluated by galvanostatic charge-discharge (GC) measurements. Three electrode cells were assembled using HC as the working electrode and Na metal as both counter and reference electrode (10 mm diameter). Two fiber glass disks (10 mm diameter), embedded by the IL electrolyte, were used as the separator. GC tests were performed at 0.1 C, 0.2 C, 0.5 C and 1 C current rates in the 0.01–2.2 V voltage range. The cycling tests were run in external environment (instead within a climate chamber) in order to point out the temperature effect on the electrode performance.</p>
            
            <p>All the electrochemical tests were carried out using a Biologic (Seyssinet-Pariset, France) multichannel battery cycler.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cssc202300840-sec-0012">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cssc202300840-sec-0012-title"> Raman characterization</h3>
            
            <p>Raman spectroscopy measurements were registered using a DILOR LabRam confocal micro-Raman with a He−Ne laser source at 632.7 nm. All samples were loaded in 3D printed holders (poly lactic acid) sealed with a 0.155 mm transparent glass window to avoid undesired reactions with air moisture. The Raman energy shift was calibrated using a Si wafer. The Raman peak fitting procedure was carried out by using Voigt function. Besides the pristine electrodes, all spectroscopic experiments were performed on samples collected post-mortem from cells in different states of charge: i) pristine material; ii) 1 discharge @0.1 C; iii) 1 cycle@0.1 C; iv) 2 cycles@0.1 C+8 cycles@0.2 C and v) 2 cycles@0.1 C+8 cycles@0.2 C+1 discharge.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
